        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:%D0%92%D0%B5%D1%80%D1%85%D0%BD%D1%8F%D1%8F_%D1%82%D0%BE%D1%87%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%BB%D1%8B%D0%B6%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%81%D1%81%D1%8B_%D0%BA%D1%83%D1%80%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%82%D0%B0_%D0%A7%D0%B8%D0%BC%D0%B1%D1%83%D0%BB%D0%B0%D0%BA..JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A ski resort in Almaty
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Kazakhstan is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_area" title="List of countries and dependencies by area">ninth-largest country by area</a> and the largest landlocked country. Today, tourism is not a major component of the economy. As of 2014, tourism has accounted for 0.3% of Kazakhstan's GDP, but the government has plans to increase it to 3% by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-theguardian1_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theguardian1-170">[164]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[165]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/World_Economic_Forum" title="World Economic Forum">World Economic Forum</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Travel_and_Tourism_Competitiveness_Report" title="Travel and Tourism Competitiveness Report">Travel and Tourism Competitiveness Report</a> 2017, travel and tourism industry GDP in Kazakhstan is $3.08&nbsp;billion or 1.6 percent of total GDP. The WEF ranks Kazakhstan 80th in its 2019 report.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[166]</a></sup> Kazakhstan received 6.5 million tourists in 2016. </p>
      <p>In 2017, Kazakhstan ranked 43rd in the world in terms of number of tourist arrivals. In 2000 total 1.47&nbsp;million international tourists visited Kazakhstan, which was increased to 4.81&nbsp;million in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[167]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a> describes tourism in Kazakhstan as, "hugely underdeveloped", despite the attractions of the country's dramatic mountain, lake and desert landscapes.<sup id="cite_ref-Lillis_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lillis-174">[168]</a></sup> Factors hampering an increase in tourist visits are said to include high prices, "shabby infrastructure", "poor service" and the logistical difficulties of travel in a geographically enormous, underdeveloped country.<sup id="cite_ref-Lillis_174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lillis-174">[168]</a></sup> Even for locals, going for holiday abroad may cost only half the price of taking a holiday in Kazakhstan.<sup id="cite_ref-Lillis_174-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lillis-174">[168]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Kazakh Government, long characterised as authoritarian with a history of human rights abuses and suppression of political opposition,<sup id="cite_ref-time_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time-21">[15]</a></sup> has started an initiative named the "Tourism Industry Development Plan 2020". This initiative aims to establish five tourism clusters in Kazakhstan: <a href="/wiki/Nur-Sultan" title="Nur-Sultan">Nur-Sultan</a> city, <a href="/wiki/Almaty" title="Almaty">Almaty</a> city, <a href="/wiki/East_Kazakhstan_Region" title="East Kazakhstan Region">East Kazakhstan</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Kazakhstan_Region" class="mw-redirect" title="South Kazakhstan Region">South Kazakhstan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/West_Kazakhstan_Region" title="West Kazakhstan Region">West Kazakhstan</a> Oblasts. It also seeks investment of $4&nbsp;billion and the creation of 300,000 new jobs in the tourism industry by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[169]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lillis_174-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lillis-174">[168]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Kazakhstan offers a permanent visa-free regime for up to 90 days to citizens of <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>, Georgia, <a href="/wiki/Moldova" title="Moldova">Moldova</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>, Russia and <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> and for up to 30 days to citizens of <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/UAE" class="mw-redirect" title="UAE">UAE</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-tim_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tim-176">[170]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mfa.kz_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mfa.kz-177">[171]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Kazakhstan established a visa-free regime for citizens of 54 countries, including European Union and OECD member states, the US, Japan, Mexico, Australia and New Zealand.<sup id="cite_ref-tim_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tim-176">[170]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mfa.kz_177-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mfa.kz-177">[171]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Green_economy">Green economy</span></h3> 
      <p>Kazakhstan launched the Green Economy Plan in 2013. According to the Green Economy Plan, Kazakhstan committed to meet 50% of its energy needs from alternative and renewable sources by 2050.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[172]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The government has set the goals that a transition to the Green Economy in Kazakhstan occur by 2050. The green economy is projected to increase GDP by 3% and create more than 500,000 new jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[173]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Expo_2017_%22Nur_Alem%22_Pavilion_Glass_Pathway_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Expo_2017_%22Nur_Alem%22_Pavilion_Glass_Pathway_2.jpg/150px-Expo_2017_%22Nur_Alem%22_Pavilion_Glass_Pathway_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Expo_2017_%22Nur_Alem%22_Pavilion_Glass_Pathway_2.jpg/225px-Expo_2017_%22Nur_Alem%22_Pavilion_Glass_Pathway_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Expo_2017_%22Nur_Alem%22_Pavilion_Glass_Pathway_2.jpg/300px-Expo_2017_%22Nur_Alem%22_Pavilion_Glass_Pathway_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="4608"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Expo_2017_%22Nur_Alem%22_Pavilion_Glass_Pathway_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Astana <a href="/wiki/Expo_2017" title="Expo 2017">Expo 2017</a> "Nur Alem" Pavilion
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The government of Kazakhstan has set prices for energy produced from renewable sources. The price of 1 kilowatt-hour for energy produced by wind power plants was set at 22.68 tenge ($0.12). The price for 1 kilowatt-hour produced by small hydro-power plants is 16.71 tenge ($0.09), and from biogas plants 32.23 tenge ($0.18).<sup id="cite_ref-renewableprices_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-renewableprices-180">[174]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_direct_investment">Foreign direct investment</span></h3> 
      <p>Kazakhstan has attracted $330&nbsp;billion in foreign direct investment (FDI) from more than 120 countries since its independence.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[175]</a></sup> According to the US State Department, Kazakhstan is widely considered to have the best investment climate in the region.<sup id="cite_ref-2013ICS_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2013ICS-182">[176]</a></sup> In 2002 the country became the first sovereign in the former Soviet Union to receive an investment-grade credit rating from an international credit rating agency. Foreign direct investment (FDI) plays a more significant role in the national economy than in most other former Soviet republics.<sup id="cite_ref-kzbankstats_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kzbankstats-183">[177]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>President Nazarbayev signed into law tax concessions to promote foreign direct investment which include a ten-year exemption from corporation tax, an eight-year exemption from property tax, and a ten-year freeze on most other taxes.<sup id="cite_ref-taxnews_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taxnews-184">[178]</a></sup> Other incentives include a refund on capital investments of up to 30 percent once a production facility is in operation.<sup id="cite_ref-taxnews_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taxnews-184">[178]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Sir <a href="/wiki/Suma_Chakrabarti" title="Suma Chakrabarti">Suma Chakrabarti</a>, the president of the <a href="/wiki/European_Bank_of_Reconstruction_and_Development" class="mw-redirect" title="European Bank of Reconstruction and Development">European Bank of Reconstruction and Development</a> (EBRD), cochaired the Kazakhstan Foreign Investors’ Council with President Nursultan Nazarbayev.<sup id="cite_ref-SirSuma_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SirSuma-185">[179]</a></sup> In May 2014, the EBRD and government of Kazakhstan created the Partnership for Re-Energizing the Reform Process in Kazakhstan to work with international financial institutions to channel US$2.7&nbsp;billion provided by the Kazakh government into important sectors of Kazakhstan's economy.<sup id="cite_ref-EBRDprrp_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBRDprrp-186">[180]</a></sup> The partnership will boost investment and drive forward reforms in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-EBRDprrp_186-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBRDprrp-186">[180]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As of May 2014, Kazakhstan attracted $190&nbsp;billion in gross foreign investments since its independence in 1991 and it leads the CIS countries in terms of FDI attracted per capita.<sup id="cite_ref-AstanaT_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AstanaT-187">[181]</a></sup> One of the factors that attract foreign direct investments is country's political stability. According to the World Bank's report, Kazakhstan is among the top 40% of countries in the world that are considered the most politically stable and free of violence.<sup id="cite_ref-wgi_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wgi-188">[182]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Kazakhstan also received high ratings in a survey conducted by <a href="/wiki/Ernst_%26_Young" title="Ernst &amp; Young">Ernst &amp; Young</a> in 2014. According to EY's 2014 Kazakhstan Attractiveness Survey, "Investor confidence in Kazakhstan’s potential is also at an all-time high with 47.3% of respondents expecting Kazakhstan to become increasingly attractive over the next three years."<sup id="cite_ref-EY2014_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EY2014-189">[183]</a></sup> The high level of economic, political and social stability and Kazakhstan's competitive corporate tax rate were the primary reasons mentioned for its attractiveness.<sup id="cite_ref-EY2014_189-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EY2014-189">[183]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The OECD 2017 Investment Policy Review noted that "great strides" have been made to open up opportunities to foreign investors and improving policy to attract FDI.<sup id="cite_ref-oecdkzpolicyreview_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oecdkzpolicyreview-190">[184]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Banking">Banking</span></h3> 
      <p>The banking industry of the Republic of Kazakhstan experienced a pronounced boom and bust cycle over 2000s decade. After several years of rapid expansion in the mid-2000s, the banking industry collapsed in 2008. Several large banking groups, including <a href="/wiki/BTA_Bank" title="BTA Bank">BTA Bank</a> J.S.C. and Alliance Bank, defaulted soon after. Since then, the industry has shrunk and been restructured, with system-wide loans dropping to 39% of GDP in 2011 from 59% in 2007. Although the Russian and Kazakhstani banking systems share several common features, there are also some fundamental differences. Banks in Kazakhstan have experienced a lengthy period of political stability and economic growth. Together with a rational approach to banking and finance policy, this has helped push Kazakhstan's banking system to a higher level of development. Banking technology and personnel qualifications alike are stronger in Kazakhstan than in Russia. On the negative side, past stability in Kazakhstan arose from the concentration of virtually all political power in the hands of a single individual – the key factor in any assessment of system or country risk. The potential is there for serious disturbances if and when authority passes into new hands.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[185]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bond_market">Bond market</span></h3> 
      <p>In October 2014, Kazakhstan introduced its first overseas dollar bonds in 14 years.<sup id="cite_ref-bloom1_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloom1-192">[186]</a></sup> Kazakhstan issued $2.5&nbsp;billion of 10- and 30-year bonds on 5 October 2014, in what was the nation's first dollar-denominated overseas sale since 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-bloom1_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloom1-192">[186]</a></sup> Kazakhstan sold $1.5&nbsp;billion of 10-year dollar bonds to yield 1.5 percentage points above midswaps and $1&nbsp;billion of 30-year debt at two percentage points over midswaps.<sup id="cite_ref-bloom1_192-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloom1-192">[186]</a></sup> The country drew bids for $11&nbsp;billion.<sup id="cite_ref-bloom1_192-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloom1-192">[186]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Housing_market">Housing market</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:TV-Turm_Almaty_-_3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/TV-Turm_Almaty_-_3.jpg/220px-TV-Turm_Almaty_-_3.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/TV-Turm_Almaty_-_3.jpg/330px-TV-Turm_Almaty_-_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/TV-Turm_Almaty_-_3.jpg/440px-TV-Turm_Almaty_-_3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:TV-Turm_Almaty_-_3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Almaty" title="Almaty">Almaty</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The housing market of Kazakhstan has grown since 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-rank1_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rank1-193">[187]</a></sup> In 2013, the total housing area in Kazakhstan amounted to 336.1&nbsp;million square metres (3,618&nbsp;million square feet).<sup id="cite_ref-rank1_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rank1-193">[187]</a></sup> The housing stock rose over the year to 32.7&nbsp;million <a href="/wiki/Square_(unit)" title="Square (unit)">squares</a>, which is nearly an 11% increase.<sup id="cite_ref-rank1_193-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rank1-193">[187]</a></sup> Between 2012 and 2013, the living area per Kazakh citizen rose from 19.6 to 20.9 square metres (211 to 225 square feet).<sup id="cite_ref-rank1_193-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rank1-193">[187]</a></sup> The urban areas concentrate 62.5 percent of the country's housing stock.<sup id="cite_ref-rank1_193-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rank1-193">[187]</a></sup> The UN's recommended standard for housing stands at 30 square metres (320 square feet) per person.<sup id="cite_ref-rank1_193-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rank1-193">[187]</a></sup> Kazakhstan will be able to reach the UN standards by 2019 or 2020, if in the medium term the housing growth rate remains within 7 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-rank1_193-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rank1-193">[187]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id=".22Nurly_Jol.22_economic_policy"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="&quot;Nurly_Jol&quot;_economic_policy">"Nurly Jol" economic policy</span></h3> 
      <p>On 11 November 2014, President of Kazakhstan <a href="/wiki/Nursultan_Nazarbayev" title="Nursultan Nazarbayev">Nursultan Nazarbayev</a> delivered an unexpected state-of-the-nation address in Nur-Sultan at an extended session of the Political Council of the Nur Otan party, introducing a "Nurly Jol" (Bright Path), a new economic policy that implies massive state investment in infrastructure over the next several years.<sup id="cite_ref-NZ1_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NZ1-194">[188]</a></sup> The "Nurly Zhol" policy is accepted as preventive measures needed to help steer the economy towards sustainable growth in the context of the modern global economic and geopolitical challenges, such as the 25%-reduction in the oil price, reciprocal sanctions between the West and Russia over Ukraine, etc.<sup id="cite_ref-NZ1_194-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NZ1-194">[188]</a></sup> The policy embraces all aspects of economic growth, including finances, industry and social welfare, but especially emphasises investments into the development of infrastructure and construction works.<sup id="cite_ref-NZ1_194-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NZ1-194">[188]</a></sup> Given recent decreases in revenues from the export of raw materials, funds will be used from Kazakhstan's National Fund.<sup id="cite_ref-NZ1_194-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NZ1-194">[188]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_competitiveness">Economic competitiveness</span></h3> 
      <p>In the 2020 Doing Business Report by the World Bank, Kazakhstan ranked 25th globally and as the number one best country globally for protecting minority investors’ rights.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[189]</a></sup> Kazakhstan achieved its goal of entering the top 50 most competitive countries in 2013, and has maintained its position in the 2014–2015 World Economic Forum <a href="/wiki/Global_Competitiveness_Report" title="Global Competitiveness Report">Global Competitiveness Report</a> that was published at the beginning of September 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-AT1_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AT1-196">[190]</a></sup> Kazakhstan is ahead of other states in the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Independent_States" title="Commonwealth of Independent States">CIS</a> in almost all of the report's pillars of competitiveness, including institutions, infrastructure, macroeconomic environment, higher education and training, goods market efficiency, labour market development, financial market development, technological readiness, market size, business sophistication and innovation, lagging behind only in the category of health and primary education.<sup id="cite_ref-AT1_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AT1-196">[190]</a></sup> The Global Competitiveness Index gives a score from 1 to 7 in each of these pillars, and Kazakhstan earned an overall score of 4.4.<sup id="cite_ref-AT1_196-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AT1-196">[190]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Corruption">Corruption</span></h3> 
      <p>In 2005, the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> listed Kazakhstan as a corruption hotspot, on a par with <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[191]</a></sup> In 2012, Kazakhstan ranked low in an index of the least corrupt countries<sup id="cite_ref-OECD1_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD1-198">[192]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/World_Economic_Forum" title="World Economic Forum">World Economic Forum</a> listed corruption as the biggest problem in doing business in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD1_198-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD1-198">[192]</a></sup> A 2017 OECD report on Kazakhstan indicated that Kazakhstan has reformed laws with regard to the civil service, judiciary, instruments to prevent corruption, <a href="/wiki/Access_to_information" class="mw-redirect" title="Access to information">access to information</a>, and prosecuting corruption.<sup id="cite_ref-oecdreport2017_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oecdreport2017-199">[193]</a></sup> Kazakhstan has implemented anticorruption reforms that have been recognized by organizations like Transparency International.<sup id="cite_ref-tpi_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tpi-200">[194]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2011 Switzerland confiscated US$48&nbsp;million in Kazakhstani assets from Swiss bank accounts, as a result of a bribery investigation in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-basel_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-basel-201">[195]</a></sup> US officials believed the funds represented bribes paid by American officials to Kazakhstani officials in exchange for oil or prospecting rights in Kazakhstan. Proceedings eventually involved US$84&nbsp;million in the US and another US$60&nbsp;million in Switzerland<sup id="cite_ref-basel_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-basel-201">[195]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">Federal Bureau of Investigation</a> and the Kazakh Anti-Corruption Agency signed a Mutual Legal Assistance Treaty in February 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-MLATfeb15_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLATfeb15-202">[196]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_and_technology">Science and technology</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Science_and_technology_in_Kazakhstan" title="Science and technology in Kazakhstan">Science and technology in Kazakhstan</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Trends_in_GERD_GDP_ratio_in_Central_Asia,_2001%E2%88%922013.svg" title="File:Trends in GERD GDP ratio in Central Asia, 2001−2013.svg"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Trends_in_GERD_GDP_ratio_in_Central_Asia%2C_2001%E2%88%922013.svg/220px-Trends_in_GERD_GDP_ratio_in_Central_Asia%2C_2001%E2%88%922013.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="309" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Trends_in_GERD_GDP_ratio_in_Central_Asia%2C_2001%E2%88%922013.svg/330px-Trends_in_GERD_GDP_ratio_in_Central_Asia%2C_2001%E2%88%922013.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Trends_in_GERD_GDP_ratio_in_Central_Asia%2C_2001%E2%88%922013.svg/440px-Trends_in_GERD_GDP_ratio_in_Central_Asia%2C_2001%E2%88%922013.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="316" data-file-height="444"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Trends_in_GERD_GDP_ratio_in_Central_Asia,_2001%E2%88%922013.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Trends in research expenditure in Central Asia, as a percentage of GDP, 2001–2013. Source: UNESCO Science Report: 2030 (2015), Figure 14.3
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Research remains largely concentrated in Kazakhstan's largest city and former capital, Almaty, home to 52% of research personnel. Public research is largely confined to institutes, with universities making only a token contribution. Research institutes receive their funding from national research councils under the umbrella of the Ministry of Education and Science. Their output, however, tends to be disconnected from market needs. In the business sector, few industrial enterprises conduct research themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-:02_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-203">[197]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-204">[198]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:The_irradiation_experiments_at_the_ion_accelerator_DC-60._Astana,_Kazakhstan.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/The_irradiation_experiments_at_the_ion_accelerator_DC-60._Astana%2C_Kazakhstan.JPG/150px-The_irradiation_experiments_at_the_ion_accelerator_DC-60._Astana%2C_Kazakhstan.JPG" decoding="async" width="150" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/The_irradiation_experiments_at_the_ion_accelerator_DC-60._Astana%2C_Kazakhstan.JPG/225px-The_irradiation_experiments_at_the_ion_accelerator_DC-60._Astana%2C_Kazakhstan.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/The_irradiation_experiments_at_the_ion_accelerator_DC-60._Astana%2C_Kazakhstan.JPG/300px-The_irradiation_experiments_at_the_ion_accelerator_DC-60._Astana%2C_Kazakhstan.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="5472"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:The_irradiation_experiments_at_the_ion_accelerator_DC-60._Astana,_Kazakhstan.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Group of Kazakhstan physicists in collaboration with Uzbek researchers working at the ion accelerator DC-60
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>One of the most ambitious targets of the State Programme for Accelerated Industrial and Innovative Development adopted in 2010 is to raise the country's level of expenditure on research and development to 1% of GDP by 2015. By 2013, this ratio stood at 0.18% of GDP. It will be difficult to reach the target as long as economic growth remains strong. Since 2005, the economy has grown faster (by 6% in 2013) than gross domestic expenditure on research and development, which only progressed from PPP$598&nbsp;million to PPP$714&nbsp;million between 2005 and 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_204-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-204">[198]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Innovation expenditure more than doubled in Kazakhstan between 2010 and 2011, representing KZT 235&nbsp;billion (<i>circa</i> US$1.6&nbsp;billion), or around 1.1% of GDP. Some 11% of the total was spent on research and development. This compares with about 40–70% of innovation expenditure in developed countries. This augmentation was due to a sharp rise in product design and the introduction of new services and production methods over this period, to the detriment of the acquisition of machinery and equipment, which has traditionally made up the bulk of Kazakhstan's innovation expenditure. Training costs represented just 2% of innovation expenditure, a much lower share than in developed countries.<sup id="cite_ref-:02_203-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-203">[197]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_204-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-204">[198]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In December 2012, President&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Nursultan_Nazarbayev" title="Nursultan Nazarbayev">Nursultan Nazarbayev</a>&nbsp;announced the&nbsp;<i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kazakhstan_2050_Strategy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Kazakhstan 2050 Strategy (page does not exist)">Kazakhstan 2050 Strategy</a></i>&nbsp;with the slogan "Strong Business, Strong State." This pragmatic strategy proposes sweeping socio-economic and political reforms to hoist Kazakhstan among the top 30 economies by 2050. In this document, Kazakhstan gives itself 15 years to evolve into a knowledge economy. New sectors are to be created during each five-year plan. The first of these, covering the years 2010–2014, focused on developing industrial capacity in car manufacturing, aircraft engineering and the production of locomotives, passenger and cargo railroad cars. During the second five-year plan to 2019, the goal is to develop export markets for these products. To enable Kazakhstan to enter the world market of geological exploration, the country intends to increase the efficiency of traditional extractive sectors such as oil and gas. It also intends to develop rare earth metals, given their importance for electronics, laser technology, communication and medical equipment. The second five-year plan coincides with the development of the <i>Business 2020</i> roadmap for small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs), which makes provision for the allocation of grants to SMEs in the regions and for microcredit. The government and the National Chamber of Entrepreneurs also plan to develop an effective mechanism to help start-ups.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_204-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-204">[198]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Soyuz_expedition_19_launch_pad.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Soyuz_expedition_19_launch_pad.jpg/300px-Soyuz_expedition_19_launch_pad.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Soyuz_expedition_19_launch_pad.jpg/450px-Soyuz_expedition_19_launch_pad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Soyuz_expedition_19_launch_pad.jpg/600px-Soyuz_expedition_19_launch_pad.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1965"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Soyuz_expedition_19_launch_pad.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Baikonur_Cosmodrome" title="Baikonur Cosmodrome">Baikonur Cosmodrome</a> is the world's oldest and largest operational <a href="/wiki/Spaceport" title="Spaceport">spaceport</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>During subsequent five-year plans to 2050, new industries will be established in fields such as mobile, multi-media, nano- and space technologies, robotics, genetic engineering and alternative energy. Food processing enterprises will be developed with an eye to turning the country into a major regional exporter of beef, dairy and other agricultural products. Low-return, water-intensive crop varieties will be replaced with vegetable, oil and fodder products. As part of the shift to a "green economy" by 2030, 15% of acreage will be cultivated with water-saving technologies. Experimental agrarian and innovational clusters will be established and drought-resistant <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_crops" title="Genetically modified crops">genetically modified crops</a> developed.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_204-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-204">[198]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kazakhstan_2050_Strategy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Kazakhstan 2050 Strategy (page does not exist)">Kazakhstan 2050 Strategy</a> fixes a target of devoting 3% of GDP to research and development by 2050 to allow for the development of new high-tech sectors.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_204-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-204">[198]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Digital_Kazakhstan">Digital Kazakhstan</span></h3> 
      <p>The Digital Kazakhstan program was launched in 2018 to boost the country's economic growth through the implementation of digital technologies. Kazakhstan's digitization efforts generated 800 billion tenge (US$1.97 billion) in two years. The program helped create 120,000 jobs and attracted 32.8 billion tenge (US$80.7 million) of investment into the country. </p>
      <p>82% of all public services became automated as part of the Digital Kazakhstan program.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[199]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Kazakhstan" title="Demographics of Kazakhstan">Demographics of Kazakhstan</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Kazakhs" title="Kazakhs">Kazakhs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic peoples</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_nomads" title="Eurasian nomads">Eurasian nomads</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Population_pyramid_Kazakhstan_2020.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Population_pyramid_Kazakhstan_2020.jpg/220px-Population_pyramid_Kazakhstan_2020.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Population_pyramid_Kazakhstan_2020.jpg/330px-Population_pyramid_Kazakhstan_2020.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Population_pyramid_Kazakhstan_2020.jpg/440px-Population_pyramid_Kazakhstan_2020.jpg 2x" data-file-width="757" data-file-height="507"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Population_pyramid_Kazakhstan_2020.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Population_pyramid" title="Population pyramid">Population pyramid</a>, 2020
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Central_Asia_Ethnic_en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Central_Asia_Ethnic_en.svg/220px-Central_Asia_Ethnic_en.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Central_Asia_Ethnic_en.svg/330px-Central_Asia_Ethnic_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Central_Asia_Ethnic_en.svg/440px-Central_Asia_Ethnic_en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2392" data-file-height="1504"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Central_Asia_Ethnic_en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Central Asian ethnolinguistic patchwork, 1992
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/US_Census_Bureau" class="mw-redirect" title="US Census Bureau">US Census Bureau</a> International Database lists the population of Kazakhstan as 18.9&nbsp;million (May 2019),<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[200]</a></sup> while United Nations sources such as the 2019 revision of the World Population Prospects<sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-207">[201]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_2019_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP_2019-208">[202]</a></sup> give an estimate of 18,319,618. Official estimates put the population of Kazakhstan at 18.711&nbsp;million as of May 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-Негізгі_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Негізгі-9">[5]</a></sup> In 2013, Kazakhstan's population rose to 17,280,000 with a 1.7% growth rate over the past year according to the Kazakhstan Statistics Agency.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[203]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The 2009 population estimate is 6.8% higher than the population reported in the last census from January 1999. The decline in population that began after 1989 has been arrested and possibly reversed. Men and women make up 48.3% and 51.7% of the population, respectively. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnic_groups">Ethnic groups</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_demography_of_Kazakhstan" title="Ethnic demography of Kazakhstan">Ethnic demography of Kazakhstan</a>
      </div> 
      <p>As of 2018, ethnic <a href="/wiki/Kazakhs" title="Kazakhs">Kazakhs</a> are 67.5% of the population and ethnic <a href="/wiki/Russians_in_Kazakhstan" title="Russians in Kazakhstan">Russians in Kazakhstan</a> are 19.8%.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[204]</a></sup> Other groups include <a href="/wiki/Tatars" title="Tatars">Tatars</a> (1.3%), <a href="/wiki/Ukrainians" title="Ukrainians">Ukrainians</a> (2.1%), <a href="/wiki/Uzbeks" title="Uzbeks">Uzbeks</a> (2.8%), <a href="/wiki/Belarusians" title="Belarusians">Belarusians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uyghur_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Uyghur people">Uyghurs</a> (1.4%), <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijanis" title="Azerbaijanis">Azerbaijanis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dungans" class="mw-redirect" title="Dungans">Dungans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kalmyks" title="Kalmyks">Kalmyks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chuvashes" class="mw-redirect" title="Chuvashes">Chuvashes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poles_in_the_former_Soviet_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Poles in the former Soviet Union">Poles</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[205]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Lithuanians" title="Lithuanians">Lithuanians</a>. Some minorities such as <a href="/wiki/Ukrainians" title="Ukrainians">Ukrainians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Koreans" title="Koreans">Koreans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Volga_Germans" title="Volga Germans">Volga Germans</a> (1.1%), <a href="/wiki/Chechens" title="Chechens">Chechens</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[206]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Meskhetian_Turks" title="Meskhetian Turks">Meskhetian Turks</a>, and Russian political opponents of the regime, had been <a href="/wiki/Population_transfer_in_the_Soviet_Union" title="Population transfer in the Soviet Union">deported to Kazakhstan</a> in the 1930s and 1940s by Stalin. Some of the largest Soviet <a href="/wiki/Labour_camp" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour camp">labour camps</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gulag" title="Gulag">Gulag</a>) existed in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[207]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Significant Russian immigration was also connected with the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Lands_Campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Virgin Lands Campaign">Virgin Lands Campaign</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soviet_space_program" title="Soviet space program">Soviet space program</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Khrushchev</a> era.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[208]</a></sup> In 1989, ethnic Russians were 37.8% of the population and Kazakhs held a majority in only 7 of the 20 regions of the country. Before 1991 there were about 1 million <a href="/wiki/Germans_of_Kazakhstan" class="mw-redirect" title="Germans of Kazakhstan">Germans in Kazakhstan</a>, mostly descendants of the <a href="/wiki/Volga_Germans" title="Volga Germans">Volga Germans</a> deported to Kazakhstan during World War II. After the <a href="/wiki/Break-up_of_the_Soviet_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Break-up of the Soviet Union">break-up of the Soviet Union</a>, most of them emigrated to Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[209]</a></sup> Most members of the smaller <a href="/wiki/Pontian_Greek" class="mw-redirect" title="Pontian Greek">Pontian Greek</a> minority have emigrated to Greece. In the late 1930s thousands of <a href="/wiki/Koreans" title="Koreans">Koreans</a> in the Soviet Union were <a href="/wiki/Deportation_of_Koreans_in_the_Soviet_Union" title="Deportation of Koreans in the Soviet Union">deported to Central Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[210]</a></sup> These people are now known as <a href="/wiki/Koryo-saram" title="Koryo-saram">Koryo-saram</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[211]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The 1990s were marked by the emigration of many of the country's <a href="/wiki/Russians" title="Russians">Russians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Volga_Germans" title="Volga Germans">Volga Germans</a>, a process that began in the 1970s. This has made indigenous Kazakhs the largest ethnic group.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[212]</a></sup> Additional factors in the increase in the Kazakhstani population are higher birthrates and <a href="/wiki/Oralman" title="Oralman">immigration of ethnic Kazakhs</a> from China, <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>, and Russia. </p>

      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Kazakhstan" title="Languages of Kazakhstan">Languages of Kazakhstan</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Kazakhstan is officially a bilingual country. <a href="/wiki/Kazakh_language" title="Kazakh language">Kazakh</a>, (part of the Kipchak family of <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">Turkic languages</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[218]</a></sup> spoken natively by 64.4% of the population, has the status of "state" language, whereas Russian, which is spoken by most Kazakhs,<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[219]</a></sup> is declared an "official" language, and is used routinely in business, government, and inter-ethnic communication, although Kazakh is slowly replacing it.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[220]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The government announced in January 2015 that the <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a> will replace <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cyrillic">Cyrillic</a> as the writing system for the Kazakh language by 2025.<sup id="cite_ref-inform.kz_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inform.kz-227">[221]</a></sup> Other minority languages spoken in Kazakhstan include <a href="/wiki/Uzbek_language" title="Uzbek language">Uzbek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_language" title="Ukrainian language">Ukrainian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uyghur_language" title="Uyghur language">Uyghur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyz_language" title="Kyrgyz language">Kyrgyz</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tatar_language" title="Tatar language">Tatar</a>. English, as well as Turkish, have gained popularity among younger people since the collapse of the Soviet Union. Education across Kazakhstan is conducted in either Kazakh, Russian, or both.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">[222]</a></sup> In Nazarbayev's resignation speech of 2019, he projected that the people of Kazakhstan in the future will speak three languages (Kazakh, Russian and English).<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[223]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>

           <td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;"><a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a></td> 
           <td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;">
            <div style="background:orange; width:0.2%; overflow: hidden;">
              
            </div></td> 
           <td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; text-align: right;">0.2%</td> 
          </tr> 
          <tr> 
           <td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;">Other religions</td> 
           <td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;">
            <div style="background:purple; width:0.1%; overflow: hidden;">
              
            </div></td> 
           <td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; text-align: right;">0.1%</td> 
          </tr> 
         </tbody>
        </table> 
       </div> 
      </div> 
      <p>According to the 2009 Census, 70% of the population is <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Kazakhstan" title="Islam in Kazakhstan">Muslim</a>, 26% <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Kazakhstan" title="Christianity in Kazakhstan">Christian</a>, 0.2% <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhist</a>, 0.1% other religions (mostly Jewish), and 3% <a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">irreligious</a>, while 0.5% chose not to answer.<sup id="cite_ref-2009_Census_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2009_Census-232">[226]</a></sup> According to its Constitution, Kazakhstan is a secular state. </p>
      <p>Religious freedoms are guaranteed by Article 39 of Kazakhstan's Constitution. Article 39 states: "Human rights and freedoms shall not be restricted in any way." Article 14 prohibits "discrimination on religious basis" and Article 19 ensures that everyone has the "right to determine and indicate or not to indicate his/her ethnic, party and religious affiliation." The Constitutional Council affirmed these rights in a 2009 declaration, which stated that a proposed law limiting the rights of certain individuals to practice their religion was declared unconstitutional.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[227]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Zenkov_Cathedral_Winter.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Zenkov_Cathedral_Winter.jpg/220px-Zenkov_Cathedral_Winter.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Zenkov_Cathedral_Winter.jpg/330px-Zenkov_Cathedral_Winter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Zenkov_Cathedral_Winter.jpg/440px-Zenkov_Cathedral_Winter.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Zenkov_Cathedral_Winter.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Ascension_Cathedral,_Almaty" title="Ascension Cathedral, Almaty">Ascension Cathedral</a> in Almaty
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> is the largest religion in Kazakhstan, followed by <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthodox Church">Orthodox Christianity</a>. After decades of <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_the_Soviet_Union" title="Religion in the Soviet Union">religious suppression by the Soviet Union</a>, the coming of independence witnessed a surge in expression of ethnic identity, partly through religion. The free practice of <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religious beliefs</a> and the establishment of full freedom of religion led to an increase of religious activity. Hundreds of mosques, churches, and other religious structures were built in the span of a few years, with the number of religious associations rising from 670 in 1990 to 4,170 today.<sup id="cite_ref-kazakhrel_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kazakhrel-234">[228]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Some figures show that <a href="/wiki/Non-denominational_Muslims" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-denominational Muslims">non-denominational Muslims</a><sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">[229]</a></sup> form the majority, while others indicate that most Muslims in the country are <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunnis</a> following the <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a> school.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[230]</a></sup> These include ethnic <a href="/wiki/Kazakhs" title="Kazakhs">Kazakhs</a>, who constitute about 60% of the population, as well as ethnic <a href="/wiki/Uzbeks" title="Uzbeks">Uzbeks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uighurs" class="mw-redirect" title="Uighurs">Uighurs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tatars" title="Tatars">Tatars</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-usstate_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usstate-237">[231]</a></sup> Less than 1% are part of the Sunni <a href="/wiki/Shafi%60i" class="mw-redirect" title="Shafi`i">Shafi`i</a> school (primarily <a href="/wiki/Chechens" title="Chechens">Chechens</a>). There are also some <a href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya" title="Ahmadiyya">Ahmadi</a> Muslims.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">[232]</a></sup> There are a total of 2,300 mosques,<sup id="cite_ref-kazakhrel_234-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kazakhrel-234">[228]</a></sup> all of them are affiliated with the "Spiritual Association of Muslims of Kazakhstan", headed by a supreme <a href="/wiki/Mufti" title="Mufti">mufti</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">[233]</a></sup> Unaffiliated mosques are forcefully closed.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">[234]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eid_al-Adha" title="Eid al-Adha">Eid al-Adha</a> is recognised as a national holiday.<sup id="cite_ref-kazakhrel_234-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kazakhrel-234">[228]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:%D0%90%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B0,_%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%87%D0%B5%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%A5%D0%B0%D0%B7%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%A1%D1%83%D0%BB%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BD_-_panoramio.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/%D0%90%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B0%2C_%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%87%D0%B5%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%A5%D0%B0%D0%B7%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%A1%D1%83%D0%BB%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BD_-_panoramio.jpg/220px-%D0%90%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B0%2C_%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%87%D0%B5%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%A5%D0%B0%D0%B7%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%A1%D1%83%D0%BB%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BD_-_panoramio.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/%D0%90%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B0%2C_%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%87%D0%B5%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%A5%D0%B0%D0%B7%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%A1%D1%83%D0%BB%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BD_-_panoramio.jpg/330px-%D0%90%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B0%2C_%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%87%D0%B5%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%A5%D0%B0%D0%B7%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%A1%D1%83%D0%BB%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BD_-_panoramio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/%D0%90%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B0%2C_%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%87%D0%B5%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%A5%D0%B0%D0%B7%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%A1%D1%83%D0%BB%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BD_-_panoramio.jpg/440px-%D0%90%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B0%2C_%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%87%D0%B5%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%A5%D0%B0%D0%B7%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%A1%D1%83%D0%BB%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BD_-_panoramio.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2981" data-file-height="1677"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:%D0%90%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B0,_%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%87%D0%B5%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%A5%D0%B0%D0%B7%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%A1%D1%83%D0%BB%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BD_-_panoramio.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Khazret_Sultan_Mosque" class="mw-redirect" title="Khazret Sultan Mosque">Khazret Sultan Mosque</a> is the biggest mosque in Kazakhstan
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>One quarter of the population is Russian Orthodox, including ethnic Russians, Ukrainians and Belarusians.<sup id="cite_ref-USCOIRFk2009_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USCOIRFk2009-241">[235]</a></sup> Other Christian groups include <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_Catholics" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Catholics">Greek Catholics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Protestants" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestants">Protestants</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-usstate_237-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usstate-237">[231]</a></sup> There are a total of 258 Orthodox churches, 93 Catholic churches (9 <a href="/wiki/Greek_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Catholic">Greek Catholic</a>), and over 500 Protestant churches and prayer houses. The Russian Orthodox Christmas is recognised as a national holiday in Kazakhstan.<sup id="cite_ref-kazakhrel_234-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kazakhrel-234">[228]</a></sup> Other religious groups include Judaism, the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Baháʼí Faith">Baháʼí Faith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-usstate_237-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usstate-237">[231]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>According to the 2009 Census data, there are very few Christians outside the Slavic and Germanic ethnic groups.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">[236]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Kazakhstan" title="Education in Kazakhstan">Education in Kazakhstan</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Palace_of_Arts_Shabyt.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Palace_of_Arts_Shabyt.jpg/220px-Palace_of_Arts_Shabyt.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="114" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Palace_of_Arts_Shabyt.jpg/330px-Palace_of_Arts_Shabyt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Palace_of_Arts_Shabyt.jpg/440px-Palace_of_Arts_Shabyt.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3597" data-file-height="1872"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Palace_of_Arts_Shabyt.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Kazakh National University of Arts
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Education is universal and mandatory through to the <a href="/wiki/Secondary_education" title="Secondary education">secondary level</a> and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_literacy_rate" title="List of countries by literacy rate">adult literacy rate</a> is 99.5%.<sup id="cite_ref-collegeatlas_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-collegeatlas-243">[237]</a></sup> On average, these statistics are equal to both women and men in Kazakhstan.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">[238]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Education consists of three main phases: primary education (forms 1–4), basic general education (forms 5–9) and senior level education (forms 10–11 or 12) divided into continued general education and vocational education. Vocational Education usually lasts three or four years.<sup id="cite_ref-UNEVOC_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNEVOC-245">[239]</a></sup> (Primary education is preceded by one year of pre-school education.) These levels can be followed in one institution or in different ones (e.g., primary school, then secondary school). Recently, several secondary schools, specialised schools, magnet schools, <a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(school)" title="Gymnasium (school)">gymnasiums</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lyceum#Lyceums_in_today's_education" title="Lyceum">lyceums</a> and linguistic and technical gymnasiums have been founded. Secondary professional education is offered in special professional or technical schools, lyceums or colleges and vocational schools.<sup id="cite_ref-collegeatlas_243-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-collegeatlas-243">[237]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>At present, there are <a href="/wiki/List_of_universities_in_Kazakhstan" title="List of universities in Kazakhstan">universities</a>, <a href="/wiki/Academy" title="Academy">academies</a> and institutes, <a href="/wiki/College_or_university_school_of_music" class="mw-redirect" title="College or university school of music">conservatories</a>, higher schools and higher colleges. There are three main levels: basic higher education that provides the fundamentals of the chosen field of study and leads to the award of the <a href="/wiki/Bachelor%27s_degree" title="Bachelor's degree">Bachelor's degree</a>; specialised higher education after which students are awarded the Specialist's Diploma; and scientific-pedagogical higher education which leads to the master's degree. Postgraduate education leads to the <i><a href="/wiki/Kandidat_Nauk" class="mw-redirect" title="Kandidat Nauk">Kandidat Nauk</a></i> ("Candidate of Sciences") and the Doctor of Sciences (PhD). With the adoption of the Laws on Education and on Higher Education, a private sector has been established and several private institutions have been licensed. </p>
      <p>Over 2,500 students in Kazakhstan have applied for student loans totalling about $9&nbsp;million. The largest number of student loans come from Almaty, Nur-Sultan and Kyzylorda.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">[240]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The training and skills development programs in Kazakhstan are also supported by international organisations. For example, on 30 March 2015, the World Banks' Group of Executive Directors approved a $100&nbsp;million loan for the Skills and Job project in Kazakhstan.<sup id="cite_ref-WB11_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WB11-247">[241]</a></sup> The project aims to provide relevant training to unemployed, unproductively self-employed, and current employees in need of training.<sup id="cite_ref-WB11_247-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WB11-247">[241]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Kazakhstan" title="Culture of Kazakhstan">Culture of Kazakhstan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazakh_clothing" title="Kazakh clothing">Kazakh clothing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kazakh_wedding_ceremony" title="Kazakh wedding ceremony">Kazakh wedding ceremony</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Equestrian_heritage,_Kazakhstan.JPEG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Equestrian_heritage%2C_Kazakhstan.JPEG/170px-Equestrian_heritage%2C_Kazakhstan.JPEG" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Equestrian_heritage%2C_Kazakhstan.JPEG/255px-Equestrian_heritage%2C_Kazakhstan.JPEG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Equestrian_heritage%2C_Kazakhstan.JPEG/340px-Equestrian_heritage%2C_Kazakhstan.JPEG 2x" data-file-width="1152" data-file-height="1728"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Equestrian_heritage,_Kazakhstan.JPEG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A Kazakhstan performer demonstrates the long equestrian heritage as part of the gala concert during the opening ceremonies of the Central Asian Peacekeeping Battalion
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Before the Russian colonisation, the Kazakhs had a highly developed culture based on their nomadic pastoral economy. <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> was introduced into the region with the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a> in the 8th century. It initially took hold in the southern parts of <a href="/wiki/Turkestan" title="Turkestan">Turkestan</a> and spread northward.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">[242]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Samanids" class="mw-redirect" title="Samanids">Samanids</a> helped the religion take root through zealous missionary work. The <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horde" title="Golden Horde">Golden Horde</a> further propagated Islam amongst the tribes in the region during the 14th century.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">[243]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:GUWSatpayev.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/GUWSatpayev.jpg/170px-GUWSatpayev.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/GUWSatpayev.jpg/255px-GUWSatpayev.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/GUWSatpayev.jpg/340px-GUWSatpayev.jpg 2x" data-file-width="734" data-file-height="1113"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:GUWSatpayev.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Kanysh_Satpayev" class="mw-redirect" title="Kanysh Satpayev">Kanysh Satpayev</a>, one of the founders of Soviet era <a href="/wiki/Metallogeny" title="Metallogeny">metallogeny</a>, principal advocate and the first president of <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Kazakhstan Academy of Sciences">Kazakhstan Academy of Sciences</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Kazakhstan is home to a large number of prominent contributors to literature, science and philosophy: <a href="/wiki/Abay_Qunanbayuli" class="mw-redirect" title="Abay Qunanbayuli">Abay Qunanbayuli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mukhtar_Auezov" title="Mukhtar Auezov">Mukhtar Auezov</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gabit_Musirepov" title="Gabit Musirepov">Gabit Musirepov</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kanysh_Satpayev" class="mw-redirect" title="Kanysh Satpayev">Kanysh Satpayev</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mukhtar_Shakhanov" title="Mukhtar Shakhanov">Mukhtar Shakhanov</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saken_Seyfullin" class="mw-redirect" title="Saken Seyfullin">Saken Seyfullin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jambyl_Jabayev" class="mw-redirect" title="Jambyl Jabayev">Jambyl Jabayev</a>, among many others. </p>
      <p>Tourism is a rapidly growing industry in Kazakhstan and it is joining the international tourism networking. In 2010, Kazakhstan joined The Region Initiative (TRI) which is a Tri-regional Umbrella of Tourism related organisations. TRI is functioning as a link between three regions: South Asia, Central Asia, and Eastern Europe. Armenia, Bangladesh, Georgia, Kazakhstan, Kyrgyzstan, India, Nepal, Pakistan, Russia, Sri Lanka, Tajikistan, Turkey, and Ukraine are now partners, and Kazakhstan is linked with other South Asian, Eastern European, and Central Asian countries in the tourism market. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kazakh_literature" title="Kazakh literature">Kazakh literature</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Kazakh literature is defined as "the body of literature, both <a href="/wiki/Oral_literature" title="Oral literature">oral</a> and written, produced in the <a href="/wiki/Kazakh_language" title="Kazakh language">Kazakh language</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Kazakh_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Kazakh people">Kazakh people</a> of Central Asia".<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-250">[244]</a></sup> Kazakh literature expands from the current territory of Kazakhstan, also including the era of <a href="/wiki/Kazakh_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Kazakh Soviet Socialist Republic">Kazakh Soviet Socialist Republic</a>, Kazakh recognised territory under the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kazakh_Khanate" title="Kazakh Khanate">Kazakh Khanate</a>. There is some overlap with several complementary themes, including the literature of Turkic tribes that inhabited Kazakhstan over the course of the history and literature written by ethnic Kazakhs. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:USSR_stamp_A.Qunanbayuli_1965_4k.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/USSR_stamp_A.Qunanbayuli_1965_4k.jpg/220px-USSR_stamp_A.Qunanbayuli_1965_4k.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/USSR_stamp_A.Qunanbayuli_1965_4k.jpg/330px-USSR_stamp_A.Qunanbayuli_1965_4k.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/USSR_stamp_A.Qunanbayuli_1965_4k.jpg/440px-USSR_stamp_A.Qunanbayuli_1965_4k.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1756" data-file-height="1272"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:USSR_stamp_A.Qunanbayuli_1965_4k.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>1965 post mark of <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> honouring Kazakh essayist and poet <a href="/wiki/Abai_Qunanbaiuly" title="Abai Qunanbaiuly">Abai Qunanbaiuly</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>According to Chinese written sources of 6th–8th centuries CE, Turkic tribes of Kazakhstan had oral poetry tradition. These came from earlier periods, and were primarily transmitted by <a href="/wiki/Bard" title="Bard">bards</a>: professional storytellers and musical performers.<sup id="cite_ref-Nurmanova_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nurmanova-251">[245]</a></sup> Traces of this tradition are shown on <a href="/wiki/Old_Turkic_alphabet" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Turkic alphabet">Orkhon script</a> stone carvings dated 5th–7th centuries CE that describe rule of Kultegin and Bilge, two early Turkic rulers ("kagans").<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="We need a source to reference this information. (October 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Amongst the Kazakhs, the bard was a primarily, though not exclusively, male profession. Since at least the 17th century, Kazakh bards could be divided in two main categories: the zhıraws (zhiraus, žyraus), who passed on the works of others, usually not creating and adding their own original work; and the <a href="/wiki/Aqyn" title="Aqyn">aqyns</a> (akyns), who improvised or created their own poems, stories or songs.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman_250-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-250">[244]</a></sup> There were several types of works, such as <a href="/wiki/Didactic" class="mw-redirect" title="Didactic">didactic</a> <i>termes</i>, <a href="/wiki/Elegiac" title="Elegiac">elegiac</a> <i>tolgaws</i>, and <a href="/wiki/Epic_(genre)" title="Epic (genre)">epic</a> <i>zhırs</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman_250-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-250">[244]</a></sup> Although the origins of such tales are often unknown, most of them were associated with bards of the recent or more distant past, who supposedly created them or passed them on, by the time most Kazakh poetry and prose was first written down in the second half of the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman_250-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-250">[244]</a></sup> There are clear stylistic differences between works first created in the 19th century, and works dating from earlier periods but not documented before the 19th century, such as those attributed to such 16th- and 17th-century bards as Er Shoban and Dosmombet Zhıraw (also known as Dospambet Žyrau; he appeared to have been literate, and reportedly visited <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>), and even to such 15th-century bards as Shalkiz and Asan Qayghı.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman_250-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-250">[244]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Other notable bards include Kaztugan Žyrau, Žiembet Žyrau, Axtamberdy Žyrau, and Buxar Žyrau Kalkamanuly, who was an advisor to <a href="/wiki/Ablai_Khan" title="Ablai Khan">Ablai Khan</a>, and whose works have been preserved by Mäšhür Žüsip Köpeev.<sup id="cite_ref-Nurmanova_251-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nurmanova-251">[245]</a></sup> <i>Er Targhın</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Alpamysh" title="Alpamysh">Alpamıs</a></i> are two of the most famous examples of Kazakh literature to be recorded in the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman_250-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-250">[244]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Dede_Korkut" title="Book of Dede Korkut">Book of Dede Korkut</a></i> and Oguz Name (a story of ancient Turkic king <a href="/wiki/Oghuz_Khan" class="mw-redirect" title="Oghuz Khan">Oghuz Khan</a>) are the most well-known Turkic heroic legends. Initially created around 9th century CE, they were passed on through generations in oral form. The legendary tales were recorded by Turkish authors in 14–16th centuries C.E.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">[246]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">[247]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The preeminent role in the development of modern literary Kazakh belongs to <a href="/wiki/Abai_Qunanbaiuly" title="Abai Qunanbaiuly">Abai Qunanbaiuly</a> (<a href="/wiki/Kazakh_language" title="Kazakh language">Kazakh</a>: <span lang="kk">Абай Құнанбайұлы</span>, sometimes russified to Abay Kunanbayev, Абай Кунанбаев) (1845–1904), whose writings did much to preserve Kazakh folk culture. Abai's major work is <i>The Book of Words</i> (<a href="/wiki/Kazakh_language" title="Kazakh language">Kazakh</a>: <span lang="kk">қара сөздері, Qara sözderi</span>), a philosophical treatise and collection of poems where he criticises Russian colonial policies and encourages other Kazakhs to embrace education and literacy. The literary magazines <i><a href="/wiki/Ay_Qap" title="Ay Qap">Ay Qap</a></i> (published between 1911 and 1915 in Arabic script) and <i>Qazaq</i> (published between 1913 and 1918) played an important role in the development of the intellectual and political life among early 20th-century Kazakhs.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">[248]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Kazakhstan" title="Music of Kazakhstan">Music of Kazakhstan</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Stamp_of_Kazakhstan_659.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Stamp_of_Kazakhstan_659.jpg/220px-Stamp_of_Kazakhstan_659.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Stamp_of_Kazakhstan_659.jpg/330px-Stamp_of_Kazakhstan_659.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Stamp_of_Kazakhstan_659.jpg/440px-Stamp_of_Kazakhstan_659.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1188" data-file-height="903"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Stamp_of_Kazakhstan_659.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Nowruz" title="Nowruz">Nowruz</a> on stamp of Kazakhstan
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The modern state of Kazakhstan is home to the Kazakh State Kurmangazy Orchestra of Folk Instruments, the Kazakh State Philharmonic Orchestra, the Kazakh National Opera and the Kazakh State Chamber Orchestra. The folk instrument orchestra was named after <a href="/wiki/Kurmangazy_Sagyrbayuly" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurmangazy Sagyrbayuly">Kurmangazy Sagyrbayuly</a>, a famous composer and dombra player from the 19th century. The Musical-Dramatic Training College, founded in 1931, was the first institute of higher education for music. Two years later, the Orchestra of Kazakh Folk Musical Instruments was formed <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://src-h.slav.hokudai.ac.jp/publictn/46/touda/touda-eng.html">From Folklore to Soviet National Culture- The Process of Formation of "Kazak National Music"(1920-1942)-</a>. The Foundation Asyl Mura is archivating and publishing historical recordings of great samples of Kazakh music both traditional and classical. The leading conservatoire is in Almaty, the Qurmanghazy Conservatoire. It currently competes with the national conservatoire in Nur-Sultan, Kazakhstan's capital. </p>
      <p>When referring to traditional Kazakh music, authentic folklore must be separated from "folklorism". The latter denotes music executed by academically trained performers who aim at preserving the traditional music for coming generations. As far as can be reconstructed, the music of Kazakhstan from the period before a strong Russian influence consists of instrumental music and vocal music. Instrumental music, with the pieces ("Küy") being performed by soloists. Text is often seen in the background (or "program") for the music, as a lot of Küy titles refer to stories. Vocal music, either as part of a ceremony such as a wedding (mainly performed by women), or as part of a feast. Here we might divide into subgenres: epic singing, containing not only historical facts, but as well the tribe's genealogy, love songs, didactic verses; and as a special form the composition of two or more singers in public (Aitys), of dialogue character and usually unexpectedly frankly in content. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:%D0%94%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%8D%D0%BB%D0%B8%D1%8F_%D0%A2%D1%83%D0%BB%D0%B5%D1%88%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0_(%D0%B4%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%8C_1)_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D0%94%D0%B5%D1%82%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D0%9D%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%BD%D0%B5_2015.jpg" class="image"><img alt=""
